Kafalah for a Deceased Insolvent: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Ruling with ikhtilaf and analogy to mahjoor slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on kafalah for a deceased muflis and explain the disagreement of the Sahibayn, identifying the underlying usul principle.

Core Idea
A deceased insolvent has no dhimma, so kafalah is invalid; but Sahibayn hold death does not discharge debt.

This passage presents a classic Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether one can become a surety (kafil) for a debt owed by a deceased who left no assets and no surety from his lifetime. The ruling hinges on the nature of the dhimma (legal capacity) after death. Imam Abu Hanifa argues that kafalah is invalid because the deceased's dhimma is no longer considered, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), argue that death does not extinguish debt, so kafalah remains valid. Understanding this dispute illuminates how usul principles about the continuity of obligations shape fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling given by Imam Abu Hanifa: kafalah for a deceased muflis is not valid. Note the conditions: when no wealth or surety remains from the deceased's lifetime.
2
Identify the opposing view and its proof
The Sahibayn hold that kafalah is valid because death does not discharge debt. Their reasoning: if debt were discharged, a volunteer payer could not claim repayment, and the deceased would not be held accountable in the afterlife.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa's core argument: kafalah means attaching one dhimma to another. If the deceased's dhimma is no longer recognized (no assets, no pre-existing surety), attachment is impossible. He contrasts with the case of an interdicted slave (mahjoor) who has a complete dhimma due to life and intellect, so kafalah is valid even though immediate payment is hindered.
The slave analogy shows that a preventing factor (hajr) does not eliminate the dhimma, whereas death does eliminate it.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if someone becomes surety for a deceased muflis, under Abu Hanifa the surety is not liable; under the Sahibayn, the surety is liable and can be pursued immediately.
5
Extract the usul principle
The dispute revolves around whether the dhimma is a necessary condition for kafalah, and whether death definitively terminates it. Abu Hanifa emphasizes that kafalah requires a viable dhimma, implying that debt obligations are tied to the person's capacity, while the Sahibayn see debt as a separate entity that survives death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْمُفْلِس لَا تَصِحُ إِذْ لَمْ يَبْقَ لَهُ كَفِيلٌ مِنْ حَالَةِ الْحَيَاةِ لِأَنَّ الْكَفَالَةَ ضَمُّ الذِّمَّةِ إِلَى الذِّمَّةِ فَإِذَا لَمْ تَبْقَ لِلْمَيِّتِ ذِمَّةٌ مُعْتَبَرَةٌ فَكَيْفَ تُضَمُّ ذِمَّةُ الْكَفِيلِ إِلَيْهَا بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا كَانَ لَهُ مَالٌ أَوْ كَفِيلٌ مِنْ حَالِهِ الْحَيَاةِ فَإِنَّ ذِمَّتَهُ كَامِلَةٌ فَتَصِحُ الْكِفَالَةُ مِنْهُ حِينَئِذٍ وَبِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا تَبَرَّعَ بِقَضَاءِ دَيْنِهِ إِنْسَانٌ بِدُونِ الْكِفَالَةِ فَإِنَّهُ صَحِيحٌ وَقَالَا تَصِحُ الْكِفَالَةُ عَنِ الْمَيِّتِ الْمُفْلِسِ لِأَنَّ الْمَوْتَ لَمْ يُشْرَعْ مُبَرِّئًا لِلدَّيْنِ وَلَوْ بَرِئَ لَمَا حَلَّ الْأَخْذُ مِنَ الْمُتَبَرِّعِ وَلَمَا يُطَالَبُ بِهِ فِي الْآخِرَةِ بِخِلَافِ الْعَبْدِ الْمَحْجُورِ الَّذِي يَقِرُّ بِالدَّيْنِ ثُمَّ تَكَفَّلَ عَنْهُ رَجُلٌ فَإِنَّهُ يَصِحُّ وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنِ الْعَبْدُ مُطَالَبًا بِهِ قَبْلَ الْعِتْقِ لِأَنَّ ذِمَّتَهُ فِي حَقِّهِ كَامِلَةٌ لِحَيَاتِهِ وَعَقْلِهِ وَالْمُطَالَبَةُ ثَابِتَةٌ أَيْضًا فِي الْجُمْلَةِ إِذْ يُتَصَوَّرُ أَنْ يُصَدِّقَهُ مَوْلَاهُ أَوْ يُعْتِقَهُ فَيُطَالَبَ فِي الْحَالِ فَلَمَّا صَحَّتْ مُطَالَبَتُهُ صَحَّتِ الْكِفَالَةُ عَنْهُ وَلَكِنْ يُؤْخَذُ الْكَفِيلُ بِهِ فِي الْحَالِ وَإِنْ كَانَ الْأَصِيلُ وَهُوَ الْعَبْدُ الْمَحْجُورُ غَيْرَ مُطَالَبٍ بِهِ فِي الْحَالِ لِوُجُودِ الْمَانِعِ فِي حَقِّهِ وَزَوَالِهِ فِي حَقِّ الْكَفِيلِ
The insolvent deceased: kafalah is not valid if no surety remained from his lifetime because kafalah is attaching one dhimma to another. So if the deceased's dhimma is no longer considered, how can the surety's dhimma be attached to it? Contrary to if he had wealth or a surety from his lifetime, for his dhimma is complete, so kafalah from him is valid then. And opposite is if someone voluntarily pays his debt without kafalah, it is valid. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: kafalah on behalf of the insolvent deceased is valid because death is not legislated as a discharge of debt; if he were discharged, taking from the volunteer would not be permissible, nor would he be held accountable in the hereafter. Contrary to the interdicted slave who acknowledges a debt, then a man becomes surety for him: it is valid, even if the slave is not required to pay before emancipation, because his dhimma is complete in his own right due to his life and intellect, and payment is also established in general since it is conceivable that his master confirm his acknowledgment or free him, so he would be required to pay immediately. Thus when his liability is valid, kafalah on his behalf is valid. But the surety is taken immediately, even if the principal, the interdicted slave, is not required to pay immediately due to a preventing factor in his case, and that factor is removed in the case of the surety.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on kafalah (suretyship) from a deceased insolvent person (muflis). The author states the rule that kafalah is not valid for a deceased who has no surety or wealth left because kafalah means joining one dhimma to another; if the deceased's dhimma is no longer valid (since he has no assets and no surety to support it), then the surety's dhimma cannot be attached. However, if the deceased had wealth or a surety during his life, then his dhimma is considered complete, so kafalah is valid. The two disciples (Sahibayn) argue that it is valid because death does not discharge debts; if it did, the volunteer payer would not be able to claim from the surety in this life nor in the hereafter. The passage then draws a comparison with an interdicted slave (mahjoor) who acknowledges a debt: kafalah for him is valid even though he is not currently liable because his dhimma is complete due to his life and reason, and his liability may become actual if the master confirms or emancipates him. Thus, kafalah is valid, but the surety can be pursued immediately, unlike the slave.

Key Terms
الكفالة (al-kafalah): Attaching one dhimma to another for the purpose of guaranteeing a debt or obligation.
الميت المفلس (al-mayyit al-muflis): A deceased person who leaves no wealth and no surety from his lifetime.
الذمة (al-dhimma): Legal capacity or liability of a person; the entity that bears obligations and can be held accountable.
العبد المحجور (al-'abd al-mahjoor): An interdicted slave who is restricted from engaging in financial transactions due to being a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, when is kafalah on behalf of a deceased insolvent (muflis) valid?
A) Always valid
B) Valid only if the deceased left no assets
C) Valid only if the deceased left assets or had a surety during his lifetime
D) Never valid

Key Takeaway
The validity of kafalah for a deceased depends on whether their dhimma is considered intact, which is the point of contention between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Priority of Claims on the Deceased's Estate
Order of priority for deceased's estate: kafan, debts, wasaya, inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi order of priority for claims on the deceased's estate and explain the rationale (ta'lil) for each priority.

Core Idea
The estate is first for burial, then debts, then bequests from one-third, then inheritance.

When a person passes away, their estate is not simply distributed to heirs. There is a specific order of priority that must be followed to fulfill the deceased's obligations and wishes. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi rule and the reasoning behind each step, helping you understand the legal wisdom in prioritizing claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four claims in order
From the passage, list the four claims on the estate in order: kafan (burial), debts, bequests, and inheritance. Understand that this order is fixed and must be followed sequentially.
2
Understand the rationale for burial priority
The passage states that burial (tajhiz) is given precedence because the deceased's need for it is stronger than all other needs. This is an immediate physical necessity for the deceased.
Reflect on why this need is considered the strongest.
3
Distinguish debts from bequests
Debts are paid before bequests because debts are obligatory to discharge the dhimma (legal responsibility), whereas bequests are voluntary. The need to clear liability is more pressing than fulfilling voluntary wishes.
Think about the difference between obligatory and voluntary claims.
4
Explain the one-third limit for bequests
Bequests are executed only from one-third of the estate after debts. The passage says the need for bequests is stronger than the heirs' right to the entire estate, but the heirs' right to two-thirds is absolute.
Consider the balance between the deceased's wishes and heirs' rights.
5
Analyze the rationale for inheritance distribution
Inheritance is distributed last, by way of substitution (khilafa) from the deceased, for his benefit: his soul is pleased by their wealth, and they may be guided to supplicate and give charity on his behalf.
Note that inheritance is not just a transfer of wealth but also a means for the deceased to receive ongoing rewards.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَشَارَ إِلَى الثَانِي بِقَوْلِهِ وَإِنْ كَانَ حَقًّا أَيِ الْمَشْرُوعُ حَقًّا لِلْمَيِّتِ بَقِيَ لَهُ مَا تَقْضِي بِهِ الْحَاجَةُ وَذَلِكَ قُدِّمَ تَجْهِيزُهُ لِأَنَّ حَاجَتَهُ إِلَى التَّجْهِيزِ أَقْوَى مِنْ جَمِيعِ الْحَوَائِجِ ثُمَّ دُيُونُهُ لِأَنَّ الْحَاجَةَ إِلَيْهَا لِلتَّجْهِيزِ أَمَسُّ لِإِبْرَاءِ ذِمَّتِهِ بِخِلَافِ الْوَصِيَّةِ فَإِنَّهَا تَبَرُّعٌ ثُمَّ وَصَايَاهُ مِنْ ثُلُثِهِ لِأَنَّ الْحَاجَةَ إِلَيْهَا أَقْوَى مِنْ حَقِّ الْوَرَثَةِ وَالثُّلُثَانِ حَقُّهُمْ فَقَطْ ثُمَّ وَجَبَ الْمِيرَاثُ بِطَرِيقِ الْخِلَافَةِ عَنْهُ نَظَرًا لَهُ لِأَنَّ رُوحَهُ تَتَنَعَّمُ بِغِنَائِهِم وَلَعَلَّهُمْ يُوَفَّقُونَ بِسَبَبِ حُسْنِ الْمَعَاشِ لِلدُّعَاءِ وَالصَّدَقَةِ لَهُ
And he indicated the second by his saying: 'And if it is a right' i.e. the legislated right of the deceased: there remains for him what suffices his need. That is why his preparation (kafan) is given precedence, because his need for preparation is stronger than all needs. Then his debts, because the need for them is more pressing to discharge his dhimma, unlike bequests as they are voluntary. Then his bequests from one-third, because the need for them is stronger than the right of heirs, and only two-thirds are their right. Then inheritance becomes obligatory by way of substitution from him, in his interest, because his soul will be pleased by their wealth, and perhaps they will be guided through good livelihood to supplicate and give charity for him.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage outlines the order of priority for spending the deceased's property. The author explains that the first priority is kafan (shroud and burial expenses) because the need for this is urgent and stronger than all other needs. After that, debts are paid to free the deceased's dhimma of obligations. Unlike debts, bequests are voluntary and only executed from one-third of the estate after debts. The heirs' share is two-thirds, but their right is subordinated to bequests because the deceased's need to fulfill his wishes is considered stronger. Finally, inheritance is distributed among heirs, and this is considered a deputyship from the deceased for his benefit, as his soul benefits from their well-being and their giving charity on his behalf.

Key Terms
التجهيز (al-tajhiz): Preparation of the deceased for burial, including the shroud and other necessary expenses.
الدين (al-dayn): Debts owed by the deceased, to be paid from the estate before any bequests or inheritance.
الوصية (al-wasiyya): A bequest made by the deceased, valid only from one-third of the estate after debts, and not valid for heirs without consent.
الميراث (al-mirath): Inheritance distributed to the legal heirs after all prior claims are satisfied.
الثُلُث (al-thulth): One-third of the estate, which is the maximum portion that can be bequeathed to non-heirs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are burial expenses (kafan) given first priority?
A) Because they are the most expensive
B) Because the deceased's need for them is stronger than all other needs
C) Because they are prescribed in the Quran
D) Because they benefit the heirs

Key Takeaway
The deceased's estate must be spent in a fixed order: burial expenses, then debts, then bequests (up to one-third), and finally inheritance, each with a rational justification.

Muwada'a in Sale: Prior Agreement vs. Public Price
Dispute on effect of muwada'a in sale with distinction between quantity and type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute regarding muwada'a in sale and apply the distinction between quantity and type in determining the operative price.

Core Idea
Prior collusion does not automatically override public declaration; its effect depends on preserving a valid price.

When parties agree privately on a price but publicly announce a different amount as a jest (hazl), which price governs? Imam Abu Hanifa and his disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, differ. This passage from Ashraf al-Anwaar presents the four scenarios for quantity and the single agreed ruling for type, along with the reasoning behind the distinction. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to evaluate when intention versus expression controls in contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The core question: does the prior secret agreement (muwada'a) or the public naming (tasmia) determine the price when they conflict due to a jest?
2
State the positions
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the public price is binding; Sahibayn hold that the prior agreement is binding, treating the public statement as mockery.
3
Analyze the four scenarios for quantity
Follow each scenario: when parties abandon the jest, agree nothing was present, disagree, or agree to build on the jest. Note the resulting price under each view.
Scenario outcomes summarized in madhhab_dispute_map.
4
Understand the unanimous ruling for type
For type (jins), all agree the contract is valid on the named type because a sale without specifying type is invalid.
5
Examine the distinction (wajh al-farq)
In quantity, the nominal price can survive as a valid price even if the extra is a condition; in type, considering prior agreement would eliminate the price entirely, ruining the contract.
This distinction explains why the ruling differs between quantity and type.

وان كان ذالك في الجنس : ۔ (۳) اور اگر جنس ضمن میں نفاق پایا جائے بائیں صورت کہ عاقدین نے پایا دونوں اس بات پر اتفاق کر لیں کہ ہم مخلوق کے سامنے سود بینار پر معاملہ کریں گے حالانکہ میرے اور تمہارے در میان اصل پیچ سو درہم کی ہے غالبیع جائز على كل حال تو بیچ درست ہے ( ضمن مسی پر ) تمام صورتوں میں مذکورہ چاروں حالتوں میں برابر ہے کہ دونوں مذاقی سے اعراض پر متفق ہوں یا نداق پر بناء میں مشفق ہوں یا یہ صورت ہو کہ دونوں خالی الذ من ہونے میں متفق ہوں یا بناء اور اعراض میں دونوں میں اختلاف پایا جائے وجہ یہ ہے کہ ایچ بلا تعین من کے درست نہیں ہوتی اور ان دونوں نے اصل بھی میں اتفاق کر لیا ہے لہذا اس کی ضیح ضروری ہے یہ اس وقت ممکن ہے جب کہ ان کی تعیین پر انعقاد پیچ درست ہو یہ حکم امام صاحب اور حضرات صاحبین کے درمیان متفق علیہ ہے۔
وَجْهُ الفَرْقِ لَهُمَا بَيْنَ الْمَوَاضِعَةِ فِي القَدْرِ وَالْمَوَاضِعَةِ فِي الْجِنْسِ حَيْثُ اعْتَبَرَا الْبَيْعَ فِي الأَوَّلِ مُنْعَقِدًا بِالْفِ وَفِي الثَّانِي بِمَا سَيْمَا أَنْ الْعَمَلَ بَالْمَواضِعَةٍ مَعَ الْجَدْ فِي أَصلِ الْعَقْدِ مُمْكِنَ فِي الأَوَّلِ إِنْ يَبْقَى مِنَ الْمُسَمَّى مَا يَصلَحُ ثَمَنًا وَهُوَ الأَلْفُ وَاشْتِرَاطَ قَبُوْلِ الأَلْفِ الْآخَرِ وَإِنْ كَانَ شَرْطًا لَكِنْ لَأَمْطَالَبَ لَهُ مِنْ جَبَةٍ الْعَبْدِ فَلا يَفْسِدُ البَيْعَ بِخِلَافِ الثَّانِي إِذْ لَوْ أَعْتُبِرَتِ الْمَوَاضِعَةُ فِيْهِ يَعْدِمُ الْمُسَمَّى وَيُوْجِبُ خُلُوا الْعَقْدِ عَنِ اللُّمَنِ فِي الْبَيْعِ وَهُوَ يُفْسِدُ الْبَيْعَ فَلِذَا وَجَبَتِ السنمِيَةُ وَلَمْ يُعْتَبَرَ الْعَمَلُ بِالْمُوَاضِعَةِ
And they both considered the prior agreement: ... And if it is in the quantity of the price: ... If they agree on abandoning [the mockery], the price is two thousand ... And if they agree that nothing was present or they disagree, then the mockery is void and the naming is valid according to him, and according to them acting on the agreement is obligatory and the thousand which they mocked is void ... And if they agree on building upon the agreement, then the price is two thousand according to him ... And if it is in the type: ... And the explanation of the difference for them between the agreement in quantity and the agreement in type, where they considered the contract in the first to be concluded with the thousand, and in the second with what ... acting on the agreement with seriousness in the original contract is possible in the first if there remains from the named [price] something suitable as a price, which is the thousand, and stipulating acceptance of the other thousand, even if it is a condition, but there is no claim for it from the aspect of the contract, so it does not invalidate the sale, unlike the second, because if the agreement were considered in it, the named [price] would be absent and would lead to the contract being devoid of price in sale, and that invalidates the sale, therefore the naming is obligatory and acting on the agreement is not considered.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul dispute on the effect of collusion (muwada'a) in sale contracts. The issue: when parties agree privately on a price but publicly announce a different amount as a jest. Imam Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ on whether the public declaration or the private agreement governs. The text presents four scenarios regarding quantity (price): (1) both abandon the jest: the public price stands; (2) both agree no jest occurred: the public naming (tasmia) is valid per Imam, but Sahibayn enforce the prior agreement; (3) they disagree: same as (2); (4) both agree to build on the jest: again Imam insists on the public price to avoid a condition that invalidates the contract, while Sahibayn uphold the prior agreement. Then for type (jins), all agreed that the contract is valid on the nominal price because the fundamental agreement on type is necessary. Imam and Sahibayn concur. The distinction (wajh al-farq) is that in quantity, the nominal price (one thousand) can survive as a valid price, whereas in type, considering the prior agreement would eliminate the price entirely, ruining the contract. Hence, the naming is mandatory and muwada'a is ignored.

Key Terms
المواضعة (al-muwada'a): A prior agreement or collusion between parties, often in contrast to a public declaration made as a jest.
التسمية (al-tasmiya): The public naming or declaration of the price in a contract.
الهزل (al-hazl): Jest or mockery; a statement not meant seriously.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a sale where parties privately agree on 1000 dirhams but publicly state 2000 dirhams as a jest, and they later agree to build on the jest, what is Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling on the price?
A) 1000 dirhams, because the prior agreement is the real intention.
B) 2000 dirhams, to avoid a condition that would invalidate the sale.
C) The sale is void due to mockery.
D) The price is determined by the market value.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the validity of a contract takes precedence over enforcing a prior collusion that would render the contract defective, but the effect of muwada'a depends on whether the nominal price can be preserved.

Hanafi Classification of Prohibitions Under Coercion (Ikrah)
Classifies prohibitions into four categories regarding coercion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four categories of prohibition (hurmah) under coercion in Hanafi usul, identify which category applies to a given act, and apply the derived ruling using the concept of rukhsa.

Core Idea
Prohibitions under coercion are not all alike: some are fully lifted, others only permit a concession, some retain liability.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi classification of prohibitions (hurmah) when a person is coerced. Drawing from Nur al-Anwar, the author presents a fourfold division. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for correctly applying the principle of necessity and coercion in usul al-fiqh, as the ruling varies depending on whether the prohibition is intrinsic or incidental.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
From the text, list the four types of prohibitions under coercion: (1) prohibitions fully lifted (e.g., wine, carrion, pork), (2) prohibitions not removable but concession allowed (e.g., uttering kufr), (3) prohibitions potentially removable but not by coercion alone, concession with compensation (e.g., taking property), (4) the category of permissibility (ibaha) which the author states is not independent.
Category 4 is not a separate category in practice; it falls under obligation or concession.
2
Examine the dalil for category 1
For the first category, the author cites Quran 6:119 ('except what you are compelled to'). Analyze how this verse provides the basis for suspension of prohibition under dire necessity, which includes coercion.
The verse uses the term 'idtirar' (compulsion), which is broader than ikrah.
3
Understand the distinction between rukhsa and 'azima
In categories 2 and 3, the prohibition remains (hurma does not fall), but a concession (rukhsa) is granted. Distinguish between the original ruling ('azima) and the concession; the concession does not make the act permissible in essence, only tolerated to avoid greater harm.
This is key: the act is still prohibited intrinsically, but the law excuses it under duress.
4
Apply the ruling on compensation
For category 3 (taking property), note that the coerced person must compensate after the coercion ends because the inviolability of another's property remains. This shows that rukhsa does not remove liability for damages.
Compare with category 1 where no compensation is due because the object (e.g., wine) is not owned.
5
Recognize the martyrdom consequence
If a person endures coercion in categories 2 or 3 and is killed, they attain martyrdom because they chose to uphold the Shari'ah over saving their life. This highlights the ethical dimension of choosing principle over safety.
This is a recommendation, not an obligation; the coerced person is allowed to take the concession.

كَلِمَةِ الْكُفْرِ فَإنّهُ قَبيح لذاتِهِ وَحُرْمَتُهُ غَيْرُ سَاقِطَةٍ لَكِنَّهُ يُتُرَخْصَ فِي حَالَةٍ الإِكْرَامِ بِإجْرَائها فَهُوَ دَاخِل فِي قِسم الرُّحْصَةِ وَحُرُمَةٌ تَحتمل السقوط لكنها لَمْ تَسْقُط بعذر الإكْرَاهِ وَإِنْ احْتَمَلَتِ الرُّحْصَةُ أَيْضًا كَتَنَاوُل مَالِ الْغَيْرِ فَإِنَّهُ حَرَام بِالنَّص يَحْتَمِلُ سَقوط حُرْمَتِهِ وَقت الإِذْنِ وَلَكِنَّهَا لَمْ تَسْقُطُ بِعَذَرِ الْإِكْرَاهِ وَيَتَرَخْصَ فِيهِ لِدَفْع الشئرُ وَيُعَامَلُ مُعَامَلَةَ الْمُبَاحِ فَإِذَا أُكْرِهَ بِالإِكْرَاهِ الْمُلْجِي جَازِلَهُ أَنْ يَفْعَلَ ذَالِكَ ثُمَّ يَضْمِنُ قِيْمَتَهُ بَعْدَ زَوَالِ الإِكْرَامِ لِبَقَاءِ عِصْمَتِهِ فَهُوَ أَيْضًا دَاخَلْ فِي قِسم الرُّخْصةِ وَلَوْ يَتَعَرَّضُ لِقسم الإبَاحَةِ لِمَا قَدَّمْنَا أَنْهَا إِمَّا داخِلَةٌ فِى الْفَرَض أَوْ فِي الرُّحْصَةِ وَلِهَذَا أَنْ وَلَأَجْلِ أنْ الحُرْمَةَ لَمْ تَسْقُطُ فِى القِسم الثَّالِثِ وَالرَّابِعِ إِذَا صَبَرَ فِي هَذَيْنِ الْقِسْمَيْنِ حَتَّى قُتِلَ صَارَ شَهْدًا لأنه يَكُونُ بِاذِلاً نَفْسه لإعْرَازِ دِيْنِ اللهِ تَعَالَى وَلاقَامَةِ الشَّرْعِ أَللهُمَّ ادْخُلْنِي فِي زُمْرَةِ الشُّهَدَاءِ وَاسْلُكْنِى فِي عِدَة السعْدَاءِ يَوْمًا لا يَنْفَعُ مَال وَلا بَنُونَ وَلَا يُنْجِي بَاسٌ وَلَا حُصُونٌ بُحُرْمَةِ نَبِيْنَا وَشَفِيْعِنَا مُحَمَّدٍ صلى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ وَعَلى آله وَأَصْحَابِهِ وَأَهْلِ بَيْتِهِ وَأَزْوَاجِهِ وَذُرِّيَاتِهِ وَسَلَّمَ. وَحُرْمَةُ تَحتَمِلُ السقوط أصلاً : - (۲) ایسی حرمت جو کلیے ساقط ہو جاتی ہے اکراہ اور ترجمہ و تشریح دوسرے اعزار کی وجہ سے اور و حلال الاستعمال ہو جاتا ہے (یعنی اس کا استعمال کرنا حلال ہو جاتا ہے ، پس وہ اس اکراہ میں داخل ہے جس پر عمل کرنا فرض اور ضروری ہے۔ كَحُرُمَةِ الْخَمَرِ وَالمَيْتَة ولحم الخنزير - جیسے شراب، مردار اور سور کے گوشت کی حرمت کیونکہ ان چیزوں کی حرمت صرف حالت اختیار میں نص سے ثابت ہے اور حالت اضطرار میں نہیں اللہ تعالی نے ارشاد فرمایا ہے قَدْ فَصَّلَ لَكُمْ مَا حُرِّمَ عَلَيْكُمْ الأما منطرِرْتُمْ إِلَيْهِ اور واضح کر چکا ہے جو کچھ کہ اس نے حرام کیا ہے مگر جب مجبور ہو جاؤ اس کے کھانے پر اس لئے حالت مخمصہ (شدت بھوک کی حالت ) اور حالت اکر اور مجبور کئے جانے کی حالت اسکے حکم سے سنتی ہے)۔ وَحُرْمَةٌ لا تُحْمِلُ السقوط لكنها تُحتَمِلُ الرُّحْصَةَ كَاجْرَاءِ كَلِمَةِ الْكُفْر (۳) الى حرمت ایسی جو کسی حالت میں ساقط ہونے کا احتمال تو نہیں رکھتی ہے لیکن اس میں رخصت کی گنجائش ہے جیسے کلمہ کفر کو زبان سے ادا کرنا کیونکہ کفر قبیح لذاتہ ہے اور اس کی حرمت ساقط ہونے والی نہیں ہے البتہ اکبر اہ کی حالت میں اس کے اجراء کی رخصت دی گئی ہے لہذا یہ رخصت کی قسم میں داخل نہیں ہے۔ وَحُرْمَةٌ تَحْتَمِلُ السَّقوط لكنّها لَمْ تَسقُط بعُذرِ الاكْرامِ وَإِنْ اجْتَمَتِ الرّحْصَةُ أَيْضًا كَتَنَاوُلِ مَالِ الْغَيْرِ (۳) ایسی حرمت جو بعض حالتوں میں ساقط ہونے کا احتمال بھی رکھتی ہے جیسے دوسرے کا مال کھانا کیا کہ نص سے حرام ہے اسکی حرمت کے ساقط ہونے کا احتمال ہے جس وقت کہ مالک اجات دیدے مگر د کے او کے غور سے حرمت ساقط نہیں ہوتی البتہ دفع شر کے لئے اس میں رخصت دید کی جاتی ہے مال مباح کی طرح تصرف میں لایا جا سکتا ہے اس لئے اگر ایسے اکراہ کے ذریعہ جبر کیا جائے جو مسیحی او مجبور کن ہو تو غیر کا مال تناول کر چجائز ہے پھر اکراہ کی صورت ختم ہونے کے بعد اسکا تاوان دینا ہو گا کیونکہ امر اہ کی وجہ سے دوسرے کے بال کی عصمت زائل نہیں ہوتی اس لئے اکراہ کی یہ قسم بھی رخصت میں داخل ہے اس تقسیم میں مصنف نے اباحت کی قسم کو بیان نہیں کیا ہے اس لئے کہ جیسا میں نے پہلے بیان کیا ہے کہ اباحت کا مستقل کوئی مصداقی نہیں ہے وہ یا فرض میں داخل ہے یار خضت میں دولہزا اور اسی وجہ سے یعنی اس وجہ سے کہ حرمت قسم ثالث درابع سے ساقط نہیں ہوتی إذا صَبَرَ فِي هَذَيْنِ القِسْمَيْنِ حَتى قُتِلَ منار شهيدًا جب ان دونوں صورتوں میں اگر ان کے باوجود صبر کرے ( اور جبری فعل کا ارتکاب نہ کرے حتی کہ قتل کر دیا جائے تو وہ شہید ہوگا کیونکہ وہ ا۔ پن نس کو اللہ تعالیٰ کے دین کے اعزاز کے لیئے اور شریعت اسلام کو قائم کرنے کے لئے قربان کرنے والا ہے۔ اللهم ادْخُلُنِي فِي زُمْرَةِ الشهداء واستلكفى فِي عِدَة المعدل :۔ اے اللہ مجھ کو بھی قیامت سے دن شہد اسی جماعت میں شامل فرما اور سعادت مندوں کے گروہ میں داخل فرما لے جس دن مال در اولاد نفع نہ دیں ۔ اگئے اور نہ کوئی قوت و شوکت اللہ کے عذاب سے نجات دلا سکتی ہے بواسطہ ہمارے نبی اور شفاعت کرنے والے حضرت سیدنا ومولانا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وعلى آله واصحابه واهل بیته ازواجه وذرياته وسلم. يقول العيدُ الْمُعْتَقِرُ إِلَى اللَّهِ الغَنِى الشيخ أحْمَدُ الْمَدْعُو بِشَيْخ جِيُونِ بْنِ أَبِى سَعِيدِ بْنِ عُبَيْدِ الله بْن عَبْدِ الرَّزَّاقِ بْنِ خَاصَّهُ حَدَ الْحَنَفِى الْمَكِي الصَّالِحي ثُمَّ الْهِنْدِي اللَّكْنَوَى قَدْ فَرَقْتُ مِن تَمِنوِيدِ نُورِ الْأَنْوَارِ فِي شَرْحِ الْمَتَارِ بِسَابِعِ جَمَادَى الْأَوَّلِ ثناء ألف زمالة وَخَمْسِ مِن هِجْرَةِ النَّبي صلى الله عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ وَآلِهِ وَسَلَّمَ فِي الْحَرَمِ الشَّرِيفِ لِلْمَدِينَةِ الْمَنَوْرَةِ وَالْبَلْدَةِ الْمُطَهَّرَةِ وَكَانَ ابْتِدَاتُه فِى غُرَةٍ شَهْرِ الْمَوْلَدُ مِنَ الرَّبِيعِ الأَوَّلِ مِنَ السَّنَةِ المَذْكُورَة على مدةٍ كَانَ عُمْرِئ ثَمَانِيَةٌ وَخَمْسِين سننَةٌ وَالْمَرْجُو مِن جانب الله تعالى ببَرُكَة رسوله صلى الله عَلَيْهِ وآلِهِ وَسَلَّمَ أَنْ يَجْعَلَهُ خَالِصًا لِوَجْهِهِ الْكَرِيم وَيَنْفَعُ بِهِ المنتدئين وستائر المسلمين الطالبين ذوى الخُلْق العَظيم والإشفاق وَالْعَمِيم
The word of disbelief - it is evil in itself and its prohibition is not removed, but a concession is given to utter it in a state of coercion, so it falls under the category of concession (rukhsa). And a prohibition that can potentially be removed but is not removed by the excuse of coercion, even though it also permits concession, like taking another's property: it is prohibited by text, its prohibition can potentially be removed at the time of permission, but it is not removed by the excuse of coercion, and a concession is given in it to repel evil, and it is treated as permissible. So if one is coerced with overwhelming coercion, it is permissible for him to do that, then he must guarantee its value after the coercion ends because its inviolability remains. So this also falls under the category of concession. And he did not mention the category of permissibility (ibaha) because, as we stated, it either falls under obligation (fard) or concession. And because the prohibition is not removed in the third and fourth categories, if one endures in these two categories until he is killed, he becomes a martyr, because he is sacrificing himself for the honor of Allah's religion and establishing the Shari'ah. O Allah, include me among the martyrs and place me among the fortunate on a day when wealth and children are of no avail, and no distress or fortresses can save, by the honor of our Prophet and intercessor Muhammad, peace be upon him, and his family, companions, household, wives, and descendants. And a prohibition that can potentially be removed completely: (2) Such a prohibition that is completely removed due to coercion and other excuses, and it becomes permissible to use; it falls under coercion that it is obligatory to act upon. Like the prohibition of wine, carrion, and pork - because the prohibition of these things is established by text only in a state of choice, not in a state of necessity; Allah says: 'He has detailed for you what is forbidden to you except what you are compelled to.' So in a state of extreme hunger and coercion, the ruling is lifted. And a prohibition that does not allow removal but allows concession, like uttering the word of disbelief (3): such a prohibition that does not have the possibility of being removed in any state, but there is room for concession. For example, uttering the word of disbelief because disbelief is intrinsically evil, and its prohibition is not removable, but a concession is given for its utterance in a state of coercion. So this does not fall under the category of concession. And a prohibition that potentially can be removed but is not removed by the excuse of coercion, even though it permits concession, like taking another's property (3): such a prohibition that has the potential to be removed in some states, like eating another's property - it is forbidden by text, its prohibition can potentially be removed when the owner permits, but due to coercion, the prohibition is not removed; however, a concession is given to repel evil, and it can be treated as permissible. So if one is coerced with overwhelming coercion, it is permissible to take another's property, then after the coercion ends, he must compensate, because the inviolability of another's property is not removed due to coercion. So this category also falls under concession. In this division, the author did not mention the category of permissibility (ibaha) because, as I stated, there is no independent instance of permissibility; it either falls under obligation or concession. And because the prohibition is not removed in the third and fourth categories, if one endures in these two categories until he is killed, he becomes a martyr, because he is sacrificing himself for the honor of Allah's religion and to establish the Shari'ah. O Allah, include me among the martyrs and place me among the fortunate on a day when wealth and children are of no avail, and no force or fortresses can save, by the honor of our Prophet and intercessor Muhammad, peace be upon him, and his family, companions, household, wives, and descendants. Says the poor servant in need of Allah the Rich, Sheikh Ahmad, known as Shaykh Jiyun, son of Abu Sa'id, son of Ubayd Allah, son of Abd al-Razzaq, son of Khassah, al-Hanafi al-Makki al-Salihi, then al-Hindi al-Lakhnawi: I have completed the composition of Nur al-Anwar in the commentary of al-Matar on the 7th of Jumada al-Ula, in the year one thousand and five (?) of the Hijra of the Prophet, peace be upon him, in the noble sanctuary of Medina the illuminated and the purified city. Its beginning was on the first of the month of Mawlid (Rabi' al-Awwal) of the mentioned year, when my age was fifty-eight years. And the hope from Allah Almighty, by the blessing of His Messenger, is that He make it purely for His noble face and benefit through it the beginners and other Muslim seekers who possess great character and compassion.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage from the Hanafi usul work ‘Nur al-Anwar’ and its Urdu commentary ‘Ashraf al-Anwar’ presents a detailed classification of prohibitions (hurmah) in the context of coercion (ikrah). The author categorises prohibitions into four types: (1) Prohibitions that are completely suspended under coercion, such as wine, carrion, and pork – these become permissible when one is forced, as indicated by the Quranic verse ‘except what you are compelled to.’ (2) Prohibitions that are not suspendable by nature but still permit a concession (rukhsa), like uttering words of disbelief – the act remains intrinsically evil, but the law grants a concession to save life. (3) Prohibitions that could potentially be suspended (if the owner permits) but are not suspended by coercion alone; however, a concession is given to act upon them, such as taking another's property under duress – one may take it but must compensate later. (4) The author notes that the category of pure permissibility (ibaha) is not independent; it either falls under obligation or concession. Finally, if a person endures coercion in the third and fourth categories and is killed, they attain martyrdom because they sacrificed their life to uphold the Shari'ah. The passage ends with a prayer and the author's colophon.

Key Terms
رخصة (rukhsa): A concession that permits a normally prohibited act under a valid excuse like coercion.
إكراه (ikrah): Coercion that forces a person to act against their will; classified as mulji' (overwhelming) or ghayr mulji' (non-overwhelming).
حرمة (hurma): Prohibition; the legal status of an act being forbidden. Can be intrinsic (li-dhatihi) or due to external factors.
اضطرار (idtirar): Dire necessity that permits otherwise prohibited acts; broader than ikrah, includes hunger and coercion.
عصمة (isma): Inviolability; the protected status of life, property, and honor that remains even under coercion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification, what is the ruling for uttering words of disbelief under coercion?
A) It becomes completely permissible and the prohibition is removed.
B) It remains prohibited but a concession is given to utter it to save life.
C) It is never permitted under any circumstance.
D) It is obligatory to utter it to avoid harm.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of prohibitions under coercion reveals four categories: fully lifted, concession with intrinsic prohibition retained, concession with compensation, and the absorption of ibaha into other categories.
